⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذكر بعض المشهورين من الطبقة الخامسة التي أدركت بعضها بالمعاصرة وبعضها بالرؤية والمجالسة فمنهم أبو المظفر الخوافى النيسابوري رحمه الله أخبرني أبو الحسن بن أبي عبد الله الفارسي في كتابه قال : أحمد ابن محمد بن المظفر أبو المظفر الخوافى الإمام المشهور انظر أهل عصره وأعرفهم بطريق الجدل فى الفقه ، له العبارة الرشيقة المهذبة والتضييق في المناظرة على الخصم والارهاق إلى الانقطاع ، تفقه على الشيخ أبي إبراهيم الضرير وكان مبارك النفس وهذا الإمام أحمد كيس الطبع فتخرج به بعض التخرج ثم وقع بعده إلى خدمة إمام الحرمين وصحبته وبرع عنده حتى صار من أوجد تلامذته وأصحابه القدماء وكان من جملة منادمـيـه بالـلـيـالـي والأيام بطول صحبته ، ولاعتداد الإمام بمكانه وكان معجباً به وبكلامه ثم ترفع عن الاعادة في درسه فكان يدرس بنفسه وتختلف إليه طائفة توفى بطوس سنة خمسمائة وكان حسن العقيدة ورع النفس ما عهد منه هنات قط كما عهد من غيره
The mention of some of the famous ones from the fifth generation, some of whom I knew through contemporaneity and some through sight and companionship. Among them is Abu al-Muzaffar al-Khawwafi al-Naysaburi, may Allah have mercy on him. Abu al-Hasan ibn Abi Abdillah al-Farisi informed me in his book, saying: Ahmad ibn Muhammad ibn al-Muzaffar, Abu al-Muzaffar al-Khawwafi, the famous imam, the most learned of his peers and the most knowledgeable in the way of dialectic (jadal) in fiqh. He had refined and polished expression and used to narrow down the opponent in debate and drive him to defeat. He studied fiqh under Shaykh Abu Ibrahim al-Darir, and he was blessed in spirit, and this Imam Ahmad was clever by nature, and he graduated some of his students. Then after that he fell to the service of Imam al-Haramayn and accompanied him and excelled with him until he became among the most prominent of his students and senior companions. He was among his close companions at night and day due to the length of his companionship, because of the Imam's esteem for his status, and he was impressed by him and his speech. Then he rose above repeating in his lesson, so he used to teach by himself, and a group used to attend him. He died in Tus in the year five hundred [500 AH]. He was of good creed and pious in soul; no faults were ever observed from him as were observed from others.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage is a biographical entry for Abu al-Muzaffar al-Khawwafi. It begins by placing him among the famous scholars of the fifth generation, with the author indicating some he knew personally and others by sight or association. The source is a book by Abu al-Hasan al-Farisi. Al-Khawwafi's full name is Ahmad ibn Muhammad ibn al-Muzaffar. He was renowned as the most learned of his time and the most skilled in juridical dialectic (jadal). The text praises his eloquent and refined expression and his ability to corner opponents in debate. He studied under Shaykh Abu Ibrahim al-Darir and was blessed. He also trained with Imam al-Haramayn, becoming one of his closest associates due to his intelligence and the Imam's admiration. Later, he advanced to teaching independently. He died in Tus in 500 AH. He is described as having excellent creed and being pious, without any recorded faults.

Biography of Abu al-Hasan al-Kiya al-Harasi
Ibn Asakir presents the biography of al-Kiya al-Harasi, highlighting his education and teaching career.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the life and scholarly career of Abu al-Hasan al-Kiya al-Harasi, his training under Imam al-Haramayn, his role as a teacher, and his comparison to al-Ghazali.

Core Idea
A scholar's voice and clarity can surpass even the greatest minds.

The passage introduces Imam Abu al-Hasan al-Kiya al-Harasi, a distinguished scholar of fiqh and kalam. Through the eyes of his contemporary Abd al-Ghafir, we learn about his journey from Nishapur to Baghdad, his mastery of Imam al-Haramayn's method, and his reputation as a teacher. This lesson will explore his life and the unique qualities that set him apart.

Guided Path
1
Read the biographical portrait
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overall sense of al-Kiya's life and character.
Pay attention to the adjectives used to describe him.
2
Compare the two scholars
Notice the detailed comparison between al-Kiya and al-Ghazali. What does this reveal about the standards of scholarship at the time?
Al-Kiya is said to be clearer in expression, while al-Ghazali is quicker.
3
Trace his career path
Follow al-Kiya's journey from student to teacher at the Nizamiyya School, noting the roles of Imam al-Haramayn and the Seljuk vizier Majd al-Mulk.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم الإمام أبو الحسن الطبرى المعروف بالكيا رحمه الله ) كتب إلى الشيخ أبو الحسن عبد الغافر بن إسماعيل قال : على بن محمد ابن على الكيا الهراسي أبو الحسن الإمام البالغ في النظر مبلغ الفحول ورد نيسابور في شبابه وقد تفقه وكان حسن الوجه مطابق الصوت للنظر مليح الكلام فحصل طريقة إمام الحرمين وتخرج بـه فـيـهـا وصار من وجوه الأصحاب ورؤس المعيدين في الدرس ، وكان ثانى الغزالي بل أملح وأطيب فى النظر والصوت ، وأبين فى العبارة والتقرير منه ، وإن كان الغزالي أحد وأصوب خاطراً وأسرع بياناً وعبارة منه وهذا كان يعيد الدرس على حتى تخرجوا به ، وكان مواظبا على الافادة والاستفادة ثم أتصل بعد موت إمام الحرمين بمجد الملك في زمان بر كيارق وحظى عنده ثم خرج إلى العراق وأقام مدة يدرس ببغداد في المدرسة النظامية إلى أن توفى فيها
And among them is Imam Abu al-Hasan al-Tabari, known as al-Kiya, may Allah have mercy on him. He wrote to Shaykh Abu al-Hasan Abd al-Ghafir ibn Isma'il, saying: Ali ibn Muhammad ibn Ali al-Kiya al-Harasi, Abu al-Hasan, the imam who reached in theoretical reasoning (nazar) the rank of the most eminent. He came to Nishapur in his youth, having studied fiqh, and he was handsome of face, his voice matching his theoretical ability, and his speech pleasant. He acquired the method of Imam al-Haramayn and graduated with him in it, and became among the most notable of his companions and the leaders of the repeaters (mu'idun) in the lesson. He was second to al-Ghazali, but rather more pleasant and better in reasoning and voice, and clearer in expression and exposition than him, although al-Ghazali was more correct in thought and quicker in expression and wording than him. And this one used to repeat the lesson to the students until they graduated with him. He was diligent in giving and receiving benefit. Then after the death of Imam al-Haramayn, he attached himself to Majd al-Mulk in the time of Barkiyaruq and was favored by him. Then he left for Iraq and stayed for a time teaching in Baghdad at the Nizamiyya school until he died there.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage is a biographical entry for Imam Abu al-Hasan al-Tabari, known as al-Kiya al-Harasi. The information comes from a letter from Abd al-Ghafir. Al-Kiya's full name is Ali ibn Muhammad ibn Ali al-Kiya al-Harasi. He reached the highest level in theoretical reasoning (nazar). He came to Nishapur as a youth already having studied fiqh, and he was physically handsome with a pleasant voice and speech. He mastered the method of Imam al-Haramayn and became one of his top students and a leader among the repeaters (mu'idun) who re-taught lessons. The author compares him to al-Ghazali: al-Kiya was more pleasant and better in reasoning and voice, and clearer in expression, but al-Ghazali was more correct in thought and quicker in delivery. Al-Kiya diligently taught and learned. After Imam al-Haramayn's death, he served Majd al-Mulk during the reign of Barkiyaruq. He then went to Iraq and taught at the Nizamiyya school in Baghdad until he died there.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

When you explain your faith to others, do you prioritize speed and correctness over clarity and pleasantness? Will you ask Allah to grant you the quality of clear and kind expression, even if it takes more time?
?
Al-Kiya attached himself to a worldly ruler after his teacher's death. Examine your own heart: Do you seek connections for worldly gain or for the sake of spreading knowledge? What would you be willing to sacrifice to remain a teacher of truth, even without worldly support?

The Burial of al-Kiya and Elegies
Ibn Asakir recounts the burial of al-Kiya, tributes, and a long elegy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the historical account of Imam al-Kiya's burial, the tributes from his Hanafi rivals, and the poetic elegy mourning his loss.

Core Idea
Rivalry in life gives way to honor and elegy in death.

This passage from Ibn Asakir's Tabyin Kadhib al-Muftari recounts the burial of Imam al-Kiya al-Harasi, a Shafi'i scholar, attended by his Hanafi counterparts who had been his opponents. Their poetic tributes and a longer elegy highlight the respect he commanded even from rivals.

Guided Path
1
Read the account of the burial
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to understand the setting: the burial in the tomb of Abu Ishaq al-Shirazi, and the presence of Hanafi leaders.
Note the chain of narration: Ibn Asakir heard from Abu al-Fadl al-Mawsili.
2
Reflect on the Hanafi tributes
Observe the two poetic lines recited by Ibn al-Damaghani and al-Zaynabi at the grave. Notice how they acknowledge al-Kiya's uniqueness despite past rivalry.
The first line speaks of the futility of mourning; the second praises his peerlessness.
3
Analyze the long elegy
Read the elegy by Abu Muhammad al-Marandi. It personifies the dwellings of the deceased, mourning the loss of 'Alam al-Huda' (al-Kiya).
The poem uses imagery of desolation and silence to convey grief.
4
Consider the historical lesson
Reflect on how this account demonstrates the respect for scholarship across madhhab lines, even after death.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سمعت الشيخ أبا الفضل محمد بن محمد بن محمد بن الموصلي الفقيه ببغداد يقول شهدت دفن الكيا رحمه الله في تربة الشيخ أبي إسحق الشيرازي رحمه الله وحضر دفنه الشريف أبو طالب الزينبي وقاضي القضاة أبو الحسن بن الدامغانى وكانا مقدمى أصحاب أبي حنيفة رحمه الله وكان بينه وبينهما منافسة في حال حياته فوقف أحدهما عند رأس قبره والآخر عند رجليه فقال ابن الدامغانی متمثلا : وما تغنى النوادب والبواكي وقد أصبحت مثل حديث أمس وأنشد الزينبي متمثلا : عقم النساء فما يلدن شبيهه إن النساء بمثله عقم أنشدنا الشيخ أبو الحجاج يوسف بن عبد العزيز بن على اللخمي الميورقى الاندلسي الفقيه المالكي بدمشق قال أنشدنا أبو محمد المرندى الخطيب يرثى الإمام السعيد شمس الإسلام علم الهدى أبا الحسن علي بن محمد الطبري رحمه الله : قف بالديار مسائلا أطلالها مستعلماً عن رسمها أحوالها أن كان يعلم ما يقول معاهد درست و خيمت الخطوب خلالها وعفا معارفها وغير رسمها ريح تجر على الثرى أذيالـهـا طوراً وطوراً عارض متهلل كمدامعي لما رأت ترحالها ما للمنازل لا تكلم داعيا ما حالها ما ذا عراها مالها أترى لفقد إمامنا علم الهدى صمت فما أن جاوبت سؤالها
I heard Shaykh Abu al-Fadl Muhammad ibn Muhammad ibn Muhammad ibn al-Mawsili the faqih in Baghdad say: I witnessed the burial of al-Kiya, may Allah have mercy on him, in the burial ground of Shaykh Abu Ishaq al-Shirazi, may Allah have mercy on him. Attending his burial were the nobleman Abu Talib al-Zaynabi and the qadi of qadis Abu al-Hasan ibn al-Damaghani, and they were leaders of the followers of Abu Hanifa, may Allah have mercy on him. There had been rivalry between him and them during his life. One of them stood at the head of his grave and the other at his feet. Then Ibn al-Damaghani recited proverbially: 'And neither the mourners nor the weepers avail, when you have become like the talk of yesterday.' And al-Zaynabi recited proverbially: 'The women are barren, so they do not give birth to his like; indeed, the women are barren of his like.' And Shaykh Abu al-Hajjaj Yusuf ibn Abd al-Aziz ibn Ali al-Lakhmi al-Mawriqi al-Andalusi, the Maliki faqih, recited to us in Damascus, saying: Abu Muhammad al-Marandi the khatib recited to us, elegizing the blessed Imam, Shams al-Islam, Alam al-Huda, Abu al-Hasan Ali ibn Muhammad al-Tabari, may Allah have mercy on him: 'Stop by the dwellings, asking their ruins, inquiring about their traces and conditions. If only the ruins knew what they say, they are desolate, and calamities have camped among them. Their landmarks are effaced, and their traces are changed by a wind that drags its trains over the soil, sometimes, and sometimes a raincloud overflowing like my tears when it saw their departure. What is with the dwellings that they do not answer a caller: what is their state, what has befallen them, what is wrong with them? Do you think that due to the loss of our Imam, the guidance of the faith, they are silent and do not answer our question?'
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage is an account of the burial of Imam al-Kiya al-Harasi. Ibn Asakir heard it from Abu al-Fadl al-Mawsili. The burial took place in the tomb of Shaykh Abu Ishaq al-Shirazi, a famous Shafi'i scholar. Among the attendees were two prominent Hanafi leaders: Sharif Abu Talib al-Zaynabi and Qadi al-Qudat Abu al-Hasan ibn al-Damaghani. Despite rivalry during al-Kiya's life, they honored him. Ibn al-Damaghani recited a line of poetry meaning that mourners cannot help once one becomes a memory. Al-Zaynabi recited a line praising his uniqueness, saying women are barren of his like. Then the passage includes a long elegy composed by Abu Muhammad al-Marandi, recited by Abu al-Hajjaj al-Lakhmi. The elegy addresses the dwellings of the deceased, mourning his loss and describing the desolation, ending with the personification of the dwellings that remain silent due to the loss of the Imam.

Key Terms
دفن
مرثية
شمس الإسلام
علم الهدى

The Life and Legacy of al-Kiya al-Harrasi
Ibn 'Asakir's biographical notice on al-Kiya al-Harrasi's excellence and funeral.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the character and scholarly status of al-Kiya al-Harrasi as recounted by his contemporaries, including his teaching methods and the respect shown by rivals at his funeral.

Core Idea
Al-Kiya al-Harrasi was a teacher of unmatched clarity whose rivals honored him at his grave.

This passage from Imam Ibn 'Asakir's Tabyin Kidhb al-Muftari presents a vivid portrait of the Shafi'i scholar al-Kiya al-Harrasi. It highlights his intellectual gifts, his dedication to teaching, and the remarkable scene at his funeral where even his Hanafi rivals paid tribute with poetry. The narrative offers a window into the scholarly culture of 12th-century Baghdad.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
First read the Arabic passage and its translation to get a sense of the narrative flow. Notice the sequence: praise, teaching, connection to Majd al-Mulk, death, burial, and poetic responses.
2
Identify al-Kiya's qualities
Look for the three qualities that make him exceptional: correctness of thought, quickness of expression, and eloquence. Also note his teaching method: he would repeat lessons until students mastered them.
These qualities are mentioned right after the initial broken phrase.
3
Understand the funeral scene
The burial was attended by two prominent Hanafi scholars, despite past rivalry. Observe their actions: one stood at the head, the other at the feet, and each recited a verse. Consider why Ibn 'Asakir includes these details.
4
Analyze the poetic tributes
Read the two verses. Ibn al-Damaghani's verse laments that mourners are useless, for the deceased is now a tale of yesterday. Al-Zaynabi's verse declares women will never bear his like. Discuss how these poems reflect the high esteem in which al-Kiya was held.
The verses show that even rivals acknowledged his exceptional stature.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جماعة أحد وأصوب خاطراً وأسرع بياناً وعبارة منه وهذا كان يعيد الدرس على حتى تخرجوا به ، وكان مواظبا على الافادة والاستفادة ثم أتصل بعد موت إمام الحرمين بمجد الملك في زمان بر كيارق وحظى عنده ثم خرج إلى العراق وأقام مدة يدرس ببغداد في المدرسة النظامية إلى أن توفى فيها . وذكر شيخنا الشيخ أبو محمد بن الأكفانى ولم أسمعه منه قال توفى الإمام شمس الإسلام أبو الحسن على بن محمد الطبري الشافعي المعروف بالكيا الهراسي ببغداد يوم الخميس مستهل المحرم سنة أربع وخمسمائة . عطاف سمعت الشيخ أبا الفضل محمد بن محمد بن محمد بن الموصلي الفقيه ببغداد يقول شهدت دفن الكيا رحمه الله في تربة الشيخ أبي إسحق الشيرازي رحمه الله وحضر دفنه الشريف أبو طالب الزينبي وقاضي القضاة أبو الحسن بن الدامغانى وكانا مقدمى أصحاب أبي حنيفة رحمه الله وكان بينه وبينهما منافسة في حال حياته فوقف أحدهما عند رأس قبره والآخر عند رجليه فقال ابن الدامغانی متمثلا : وما تغنى النوادب والبواكي وقد أصبحت مثل حديث أمس وأنشد الزينبي متمثلا : عقم النساء فما يلدن شبيهه إن النساء بمثله عقم
A group [said]: He was one of the most correct in thought, quickest in expression, and most eloquent in speech. And he used to repeat the lesson until they would master it. He was diligent in giving and receiving benefit. Then after the death of Imam al-Haramayn, he connected with Majd al-Mulk in the time of Bar Kiyaruq and was favored by him. Then he went out to Iraq and stayed a period teaching in Baghdad at the Nizamiyya school until he died there. And our shaykh, Shaykh Abu Muhammad ibn al-Akfani, mentioned — and I did not hear it from him — he said: The Imam Shams al-Islam Abu al-Hasan Ali ibn Muhammad al-Tabari al-Shafi'i, known as al-Kiya al-Harrasi, died in Baghdad on Thursday, the first of Muharram in the year 504 AH. 'Ataf: I heard Shaykh Abu al-Fadl Muhammad ibn Muhammad ibn Muhammad ibn al-Mawsili al-Faqih in Baghdad say: I witnessed the burial of al-Kiya — may God have mercy on him — in the tomb of Shaykh Abu Ishaq al-Shirazi — may God have mercy on him. Present at his burial were al-Sharif Abu Talib al-Zaynabi and the Chief Judge Abu al-Hasan ibn al-Damaghani, and they were the leaders of the followers of Abu Hanifa — may God have mercy on him. There had been competition between him and them during his life. So one of them stood at the head of his grave and the other at his feet. Ibn al-Damaghani recited, as an example: 'And what do the mourners and wailers avail, when you have become like a tale of yesterday?' And al-Zaynabi recited, as an example: 'Women are barren, so they bear none like him; women are indeed barren of his like.'
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage is a biography of Imam al-Kiya al-Harrasi (d. 504 AH). It begins by praising his intellectual qualities: he was one of the most correct in thought (أصوب خاطراً), quickest in expression (أسرع بياناً), and most eloquent (أعبر عبارة). He would repeat lessons until students mastered them, showing his dedication to teaching. After the death of Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni), he connected with the vizier Majd al-Mulk during the reign of Barkiyaruq and gained favor. He then moved to Baghdad and taught at the Nizamiyya school until his death. The author then quotes his shaykh reporting that al-Kiya died on Thursday, 1 Muharram 504 AH. The text then describes his funeral: the narrator saw him buried next to Shaykh Abu Ishaq al-Shirazi. Two prominent Hanafi scholars attended: al-Sharif Abu Talib al-Zaynabi and Chief Judge Ibn al-Damaghani, despite having been rivals during his life. At the grave, they recited two poetic verses: Ibn al-Damaghani's verse laments that mourners are useless, for the deceased has become like a story of yesterday; al-Zaynabi's verse declares that women will never bear his like. These verses express the high esteem in which he was held.

Elegy for Imam al-Kiya al-Harrasi
Elegy poem mourning the death of Imam al-Kiya al-Harrasi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key themes and rhetorical devices of the elegy composed for Imam al-Kiya al-Harrasi, and appreciate how it reflects the author's deep reverence and the theological virtue of patience in the face of loss.

Core Idea
Elegy as praise: a scholar's death is Islam's sunset mourned, yet patience remains.

The elegy (رثاء) is a classical Arabic poetic form used to eulogize the deceased. In Islamic scholarly circles, elegies often blend intense personal grief with profound praise for the departed's religious contributions. The poem before us, composed by Abu Muhammad al-Marandi for the celebrated Shafi'i scholar Imam al-Kiya al-Harrasi (Shams al-Islam), exemplifies this genre. As you read, note how the poet uses the conventional 'ruined dwelling' opening to draw the reader into a mood of loss, then transitions to soaring praise and ends with a call to patience — thereby turning raw emotion into a lesson in faith.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the entire elegy once in Arabic, then follow the translation. Notice the rhyme and rhythm of the poem even as you grasp its meaning.
The poem is a single long qasida; read it aloud if possible to feel the meter.
2
Identify the main sections
The poem moves from the 'atiyah (opening on ruins) to the main lament for the scholar's loss. Find where the poet begins to praise the Imam's virtues, and where he expresses wonder (أعجب). Finally, note the shift to comfort and patience.
3
Explore the imagery
The poet uses powerful metaphors: the scholar is a 'sun' (شمس) and a 'sea' (بحر). What do these images convey about his stature? How does the poet's marveling reinforce his greatness?
These metaphors are common in Islamic elegies for great scholars.
4
Reflect on the theological message
The poem ends with a call to 'beautiful patience' (الصبر الجميل) and satisfaction with God's decree. This shows that despite the appearance of utter loss, the Muslim response is submission. Why is this important?
This connects to the broader Islamic teaching on dealing with calamity.
5
Answer the checkpoint question
The MCQ will ask about the poet's marvel. Confirm you can locate this in the text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أنشدنا الشيخ أبو الحجاج يوسف بن عبد العزيز بن على اللخمي الميورقى الاندلسي الفقيه المالكي بدمشق قال أنشدنا أبو محمد المرندى الخطيب يرثى الإمام السعيد شمس الإسلام علم الهدى أبا الحسن علي بن محمد الطبري رحمه الله : قف بالديار مسائلا أطلالها مستعلماً عن رسمها أحوالها أن كان يعلم ما يقول معاهد درست و خيمت الخطوب خلالها وعفا معارفها وغير رسمها ريح تجر على الثرى أذيالـهـا طوراً وطوراً عارض متهلل كمدامعي لما رأت ترحالها ما للمنازل لا تكلم داعيا ما حالها ما ذا عراها مالها أترى لفقد إمامنا علم الهدى صمت فما أن جاوبت سؤالها يا للمكارم والفضائل بعده يا للعلوم وللشـ رائع يالها يا للمحاسن والمحاضر والندا سلب المنايا شمسها وجمالها رفعت به رایات دین محمد فالآن صرف الحادثات أمالها بلوا الخدود بأدمع منهلة أن الرزية أفجعت عذالها ومصيبة حلت وعم وقوعـهـا زمر الأنام نساءها ورجالها يا محنة صدع القلوب هجومها واستنزلت من علوها آجالها دكت المصرعه الجبال وزعزعت والأرض منه زلزلت زلزالها لهفى على الإسلام غابت شمسه بعد الشروق فواصلت أصالها . أين الذي ساد البرية كلها وهدى إلى سبل الهدى ضلالها نصر الشريعة بعدما نشر الورى للمنكرات على البسيط ظلالها فاليوم تبلى في التراب محاسن لم تلف في كل الورى أمثالها و شمائل رقت فحاكت رقة قطر السحائب مازجت جریالها أني لأعجب كيف وارث تربة بحراً ولم يغرق به من هالها أم كيف يدفن في الثرى شمس الضحى والشمس يحرق حرها من نالها ماذا يقال لمعشر هجروا الكرى وتجاوزوا عفر الزبا ورمالها وتحققت نيل الرجاء نفوسهم فأبى الزمــان مـخـيـبـاً آمـالـهـا من مبلغ عنـا إلـيــه تحـــيـــة تبقى فلا يخشى الزمان زيالها ومخبر أن النفوس بأسرها شوقاً إليه تشتهى آجالها تقضى بأوراد الدعـاء حـقـوقـه ما قابلت ريح الجنوب شمالها ونعود الصبر الجميل نفوسنا إن الرضى بقضائه أولى لهـا
Shaykh Abu al-Hajjaj Yusuf ibn Abd al-Aziz ibn Ali al-Lakhmi al-Mayurqi al-Andalusi, the Maliki jurist in Damascus, recited to us, saying: Abu Muhammad al-Marandi, the preacher, recited to us an elegy for the blessed Imam Shams al-Islam, the Guide of Guidance, Abu al-Hasan Ali ibn Muhammad al-Tabari — may God have mercy on him: Stand at the dwellings, questioning their ruins, seeking to learn from their traces their state. Perhaps they know what a covenant that has decayed says, and calamities have camped within them. Their landmarks have been effaced, and their trace changed by a wind that drags its trains over the earth, sometimes and sometimes by a rain-laden cloud, like my tears when it saw their departure. What is with the dwellings that they do not answer a caller? What is their state? What has befallen them? What is wrong with them? Do you think that due to the loss of our Imam, the Guide of Guidance, they have become silent and not answered our question? O for the noble deeds and virtues after him! O for the sciences and the revealed laws! O for them! O for the beauty, assemblies, and generosity! The fates have stolen their sun and beauty. By him the banners of Muhammad's religion were raised, but now the turn of events has bent them. They have worn out their cheeks with flowing tears, for the calamity has grieved their reproachers. And a calamity has occurred, and its occurrence became widespread to groups of people, women and men. O affliction whose onslaught has cleft hearts and brought down their lifespans from on high! The battlefield crushed the mountains and shook them, and the earth was shaken by his shaking. Alas for Islam! Its sun has set after rising, so it has continued its evening. Where is he who ruled all creation and guided its straying to the paths of guidance? He aided the Sharia after spreading shadows of evils over the earth? Today, beauties that have no equal among all creation are worn away in the dust. And refined qualities that rival the delicacy of raindrops, mingling with their flow? Indeed, I marvel how a sea is buried in soil, and yet he who feared it was not drowned? Or how the sun of the forenoon is buried in the dust, while the sun's heat burns whoever attains it? What can be said to a people who have abandoned sleep and traversed the dust of sand dunes and its sands, and their souls attained the realization of hope, but time disappointed their hopes? Who will convey from us a greeting to him that remains, so that time will not fear its departure? And informer that all souls, out of longing for him, desire their lifespans? His rights are fulfilled by the recitations of supplications, as long as the south wind meets the north. And we comfort our souls with beautiful patience, for contentment with His decree is more fitting for it.
Source pages p. 222

Commentary
This passage is an elegy poem (مرثية) composed by Abu Muhammad al-Marandi in mourning for Imam al-Kiya al-Harrasi (Shams al-Islam). The poem begins by addressing the ruined dwellings (الديار) and their traces, a conventional motif in Arabic elegy, and asks them why they are silent — suggesting that the loss of the Imam has caused even the landscape to fall mute. The poet then laments the loss of noble qualities, sciences, and religious laws, saying that Death has stolen the sun and beauty of these things. The banners of Islam were raised through the Imam, but now they are bent by misfortunes. People weep and the calamity spreads. The poet uses striking imagery: the battlefield crushes mountains, the earth quakes. The sun of Islam has set. He praises the Imam as the ruler of all creation who guided the misguided and aided the Sharia. Now his beauties are buried in dust, and the poet marvels that a sea (the scholar's knowledge) can be buried in soil without drowning, and the sun can be buried without burning. He then addresses the people who have lost hope, asking who will convey greetings to the deceased. The poem concludes with a call for patience and contentment with God's decree. The language is highly rhetorical, using rhetorical questions and hyperbole to convey the immense stature of the scholar and the depth of the loss.

Key Terms
أبو الحجاج اللخمي
أبو محمد المرندي
شمس الإسلام
علم الهدى
الكيا الهراسي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This elegy expresses hopeless despair over the loss of a scholar, implying that Islam itself has been diminished permanently.
Correction: Although the poem vividly mourns the death, it ends with a call to patience and contentment with Allah's decree, affirming that faith and submission remain. The sun of Islam setting refers to the loss of a guiding light, not the faith's decline.

Checkpoint
What does the poet 'marvel at' (أعجب) in the elegy?
A) That a sea is buried in soil without drowning
B) That the Imam died so young
C) That the mourners did not recite Quran
D) That the dwellings spoke

Key Takeaway
The elegy for Imam al-Kiya al-Harrasi uses rich imagery to mourn his loss while teaching the believer to respond with patience and trust in God's decree.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you lose someone beloved to you, do your tears flow with any impatience? The poet weeped but ended with 'beautiful patience'. Can you truly say your grief is accompanied by contentment with Allah's decree, or are you merely resigned?
?
The poem says the sun of Islam set with this scholar's death. Do you live as though your personal contribution to Islam's sun is so significant that your own death would be a loss? What legacy will you leave that will be mourned as a sun setting?
?
The poet calls the scholar a sea of knowledge. How thirsty are you for such knowledge? Are you truly seeking knowledge like a sea, or are you satisfied with puddles?

Al-Ghazali's Early Life and Education
Early life and education under al-Juwayni.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the early academic formation of Imam al-Ghazali under al-Juwayni and how his brilliance became evident.

Core Idea
Al-Ghazali's intellectual gifts outshone even his teacher's.

This first part of al-Farisi's account traces al-Ghazali's early studies in Tus and Naysabur. It highlights his exceptional abilities and the complex relationship with Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni, who both admired and envied him.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on early life
Read the Arabic and translation carefully to absorb the narrative flow and key details about al-Ghazali's youth.
Note the praise of his eloquence, intellect, and speed.
2
Identify his teachers and timeline
Notice his first teacher in Tus (Ahmad al-Radhkani) and his move to Naysabur to study under Imam al-Haramayn.
This shows the traditional path of seeking knowledge.
3
Reflect on the teacher-student dynamic
Al-Juwayni's concealed jealousy despite outward pride is mentioned. Think about how human nature can affect scholarly relationships.
This nuance adds depth to the biography.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أخبرنا الشيخ أبو الحسن عبد الغافر بن إسماعيل الفارسي في كتابه قال : محمد بن محمد بن محمد أبو حامد الغزالى حجة الإسلام والمسلمين إمام أئمة الدين من لم تر العيون مثله لسانا وبيانا ونطقا و خاطرا وذكاء وطبعا ، شذا طرفا في صباه بطوس من الفقه على الإمام أحمد الراذ کانی تم قدم نيسابور مختلفا إلى درس إمام الحرمين في طائفة من الشبان من طوس ، وجد واجتهد حتى تخرج عن مدة قريبة وبز الأقران وجمل القرآن وصار انظر أهل زمانه وواحد أقرانه في أيام إمام الحرمين ، وكان الطلبة يستفيدون منه ويدرس لهم ويرشدهم ويجتهد في نفسه وبلغ الأمر به إلى أن أخذ في التصنيف وكان الإمام مع علو درجته وسمو عبارته وسرعة جريه في النطق والكلام لا يصغى نظره إلى الغزالي ستراً لإنافته عليه في سرعة العبارة وقوة الطبع ، ولا يطيب له تصديه للتصانيف وإن كان متخرجا به منتسبا إليه كما لا يخفى من طبع البشر ولكنه يظهر التبجح به والاعتداد بمكانه ظاهرا خلاف ما يضمره ثم بقى كذلك إلى انقضاء أيام الإمام فخرج من نيسابور وصار إلى المعسكر واحتل من مجلس نظام الملك . محل القبول وأقبل عليه الصاحب لعلو درجته وظهور أسمه وحسن مناظرته ، وجرى عبارته وكانت تلك الحضرة محط رحال العلماء ومقصد الأئمة والفصحاء فوقعت للغزالى إتفاقات حسنة من الاحتكاك بالأئمة ، وملاقاة الخصوم اللد ومناظرة الفحول ومناقرة الكبار وظهر أسمه في الآفاق وارتفق بذلك أكمل الارتفاق ، حتي أدت الحال به إلى أن رسم للمصير إلى بغداد للقيام بتدريس المدرسة الميمونة النظامية بها ، فصار إليها وأعجب الكل بتدريسه ومناظرته ، وما لقي مثل نفسه وصار بعد إمامة خراسان إمام العراق، ثم نظر في علم الأصول وكان قد أحكمها فصنف فيه تصانيف وجدد المذهب فى الفقه فصنف فيه تصانيف ، وسبك الخلاف فحرر فيه أيضاً تصانيف وعلت حشمته و درجته في بغداد ، حتى كان تغلب حشمته الأكابر والأمراء ودار الخلافة فانقلب الأمر من وجه آخر وظهر عليه بعد مطالعة للعلوم الدقيقة وممارسة الكتب المصنفة فيها ، وسلك طريق التزهد والتأله وترك الحشمة وطرح ما نال من الدرجة والاشتغال بأسباب التقوى وزاد الآخرة ، فخرج عما كان فيه وقصد بيت الله تعالى وحج ثم
The Shaykh Abu al-Hasan Abd al-Ghafir ibn Isma‘il al-Farisi informed us in his book, saying: Muhammad ibn Muhammad ibn Muhammad Abu Hamid al-Ghazali, the Proof of Islam and the Muslims, the Imam of the imams of the religion – the eyes have not seen his like in tongue, eloquence, speech, intellect, intelligence, and nature. He had a share in his youth in Tus of jurisprudence from Imam Ahmad al-Radhkani. Then he came to Naysabur, attending the lessons of Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni) with a group of youths from Tus. He strived and exerted himself until he graduated in a short time, surpassed his peers, and memorized the Qur'an. He became the most perceptive of his time and the unique among his contemporaries in the days of Imam al-Haramayn. The students would benefit from him, and he would teach them, guide them, and exert himself. His situation reached the point where he began authoring. The Imam (al-Juwayni), despite his high rank, lofty expression, and speed of speech, would not direct his gaze to al-Ghazali out of concealment due to his superiority over him in speed of expression and strength of nature. He did not approve of al-Ghazali's undertaking of books, even though he was a graduate under him and affiliated with him, as is not hidden from human nature, but he would outwardly show pride in him and confidence in his position, contrary to what he concealed. Then he remained thus until the death of the Imam. He left Naysabur and went to the military camp, and from the council of Nizam al-Mulk he attained a position of acceptance. The minister (Nizam al-Mulk) turned to him due to his high status, the fame of his name, and his excellent disputation and eloquent speech. That court was the resting place of scholars and the destination of imams and eloquent speakers. There befell al-Ghazali favorable opportunities from interacting with imams, encountering stubborn opponents, debating with eminent men, and contending with the great. His name became famous in the horizons, and he benefited thereby the utmost benefit, until the situation led him to be ordered to go to Baghdad to teach at the auspicious Nizamiyya school there. So he went there, and everyone was amazed by his teaching and disputation. He met none like himself, and after being the imam of Khurasan, he became the imam of Iraq. Then he examined the science of principles (usul) and had mastered it, so he authored works in it. He revived the school (madhhab) in jurisprudence and authored works in it. He refined the science of disagreement (khilaf) and also produced works in it. His entourage and rank rose in Baghdad until his entourage overwhelmed the great ones, the commanders, and the caliphal court. Then the matter turned from another direction: after his extensive study of the exact sciences and his practice of the books authored in them, he adopted the path of asceticism and devotion to God, left behind entourage, discarded what he had attained of rank, and busied himself with the means of piety and the provisions of the Hereafter. So he left what he was in and headed to the House of God Almighty and performed Hajj, then...
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage is a biographical account of Imam al-Ghazali from the transmission of Abd al-Ghafir al-Farisi. It details his early life, education, rise to prominence, and later spiritual transformation. The narrative begins by praising al-Ghazali's unparalleled eloquence, intelligence, and nature. It then describes his early study of jurisprudence in Tus under Ahmad al-Radhkani, followed by his move to Naysabur to study under Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni). He quickly excelled, surpassing his peers and becoming a leading figure even during his teacher's lifetime. The passage notes a subtle rivalry: al-Juwayni would not look directly at al-Ghazali due to his jealousy of Ghazali's superior speed and strength, though outwardly he praised him. After al-Juwayni's death, al-Ghazali left Naysabur and joined the court of Nizam al-Mulk, where his abilities in debate and eloquence earned him high favor. He was sent to Baghdad to teach at the Nizamiyya school, where he became the leading scholar of both Khurasan and Iraq. He authored major works in usul al-fiqh, fiqh, and khilaf, and his influence grew immensely. However, as the passage concludes, he underwent a dramatic spiritual change, abandoning worldly status for asceticism, devotion, and the pilgrimage to Mecca, marking a shift from scholarly prestige to otherworldly renunciation.

From Naysabur to Baghdad and Ascetic Turn
Later career, fame, and spiritual reorientation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Ghazali's career progression under Nizam al-Mulk, his teaching in Baghdad, and his eventual embrace of asceticism.

Core Idea
Worldly fame gave way to spiritual yearning.

This second part of al-Farisi's account follows al-Ghazali after his teacher's death. He gains favor with Nizam al-Mulk, teaches in Baghdad, becomes the leading scholar of his age, but then undergoes a dramatic spiritual transformation, leaving everything for the Hajj.

Guided Path
1
Read the remainder of the narrative
Focus on the sequence of events after leaving Naysabur: court life, Baghdad, authorship, and transformation.
Note the increasing levels of prestige.
2
Identify key milestones
Joining Nizam al-Mulk, teaching at Nizamiyya, becoming imam of Iraq, authoring major works.
These show his academic influence.
3
Reflect on the spiritual change
After reaching the pinnacle, he suddenly adopts asceticism and performs Hajj. Consider the reasons suggested by 'study of exact sciences'.
This is a major turning point in his life.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أخبرنا الشيخ أبو الحسن عبد الغافر بن إسماعيل الفارسي في كتابه قال : محمد بن محمد بن محمد أبو حامد الغزالى حجة الإسلام والمسلمين إمام أئمة الدين من لم تر العيون مثله لسانا وبيانا ونطقا و خاطرا وذكاء وطبعا ، شذا طرفا في صباه بطوس من الفقه على الإمام أحمد الراذ کانی تم قدم نيسابور مختلفا إلى درس إمام الحرمين في طائفة من الشبان من طوس ، وجد واجتهد حتى تخرج عن مدة قريبة وبز الأقران وجمل القرآن وصار انظر أهل زمانه وواحد أقرانه في أيام إمام الحرمين ، وكان الطلبة يستفيدون منه ويدرس لهم ويرشدهم ويجتهد في نفسه وبلغ الأمر به إلى أن أخذ في التصنيف وكان الإمام مع علو درجته وسمو عبارته وسرعة جريه في النطق والكلام لا يصغى نظره إلى الغزالي ستراً لإنافته عليه في سرعة العبارة وقوة الطبع ، ولا يطيب له تصديه للتصانيف وإن كان متخرجا به منتسبا إليه كما لا يخفى من طبع البشر ولكنه يظهر التبجح به والاعتداد بمكانه ظاهرا خلاف ما يضمره ثم بقى كذلك إلى انقضاء أيام الإمام فخرج من نيسابور وصار إلى المعسكر واحتل من مجلس نظام الملك . محل القبول وأقبل عليه الصاحب لعلو درجته وظهور أسمه وحسن مناظرته ، وجرى عبارته وكانت تلك الحضرة محط رحال العلماء ومقصد الأئمة والفصحاء فوقعت للغزالى إتفاقات حسنة من الاحتكاك بالأئمة ، وملاقاة الخصوم اللد ومناظرة الفحول ومناقرة الكبار وظهر أسمه في الآفاق وارتفق بذلك أكمل الارتفاق ، حتي أدت الحال به إلى أن رسم للمصير إلى بغداد للقيام بتدريس المدرسة الميمونة النظامية بها ، فصار إليها وأعجب الكل بتدريسه ومناظرته ، وما لقي مثل نفسه وصار بعد إمامة خراسان إمام العراق، ثم نظر في علم الأصول وكان قد أحكمها فصنف فيه تصانيف وجدد المذهب فى الفقه فصنف فيه تصانيف ، وسبك الخلاف فحرر فيه أيضاً تصانيف وعلت حشمته و درجته في بغداد ، حتى كان تغلب حشمته الأكابر والأمراء ودار الخلافة فانقلب الأمر من وجه آخر وظهر عليه بعد مطالعة للعلوم الدقيقة وممارسة الكتب المصنفة فيها ، وسلك طريق التزهد والتأله وترك الحشمة وطرح ما نال من الدرجة والاشتغال بأسباب التقوى وزاد الآخرة ، فخرج عما كان فيه وقصد بيت الله تعالى وحج ثم
The Shaykh Abu al-Hasan Abd al-Ghafir ibn Isma‘il al-Farisi informed us in his book, saying: Muhammad ibn Muhammad ibn Muhammad Abu Hamid al-Ghazali, the Proof of Islam and the Muslims, the Imam of the imams of the religion – the eyes have not seen his like in tongue, eloquence, speech, intellect, intelligence, and nature. He had a share in his youth in Tus of jurisprudence from Imam Ahmad al-Radhkani. Then he came to Naysabur, attending the lessons of Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni) with a group of youths from Tus. He strived and exerted himself until he graduated in a short time, surpassed his peers, and memorized the Qur'an. He became the most perceptive of his time and the unique among his contemporaries in the days of Imam al-Haramayn. The students would benefit from him, and he would teach them, guide them, and exert himself. His situation reached the point where he began authoring. The Imam (al-Juwayni), despite his high rank, lofty expression, and speed of speech, would not direct his gaze to al-Ghazali out of concealment due to his superiority over him in speed of expression and strength of nature. He did not approve of al-Ghazali's undertaking of books, even though he was a graduate under him and affiliated with him, as is not hidden from human nature, but he would outwardly show pride in him and confidence in his position, contrary to what he concealed. Then he remained thus until the death of the Imam. He left Naysabur and went to the military camp, and from the council of Nizam al-Mulk he attained a position of acceptance. The minister (Nizam al-Mulk) turned to him due to his high status, the fame of his name, and his excellent disputation and eloquent speech. That court was the resting place of scholars and the destination of imams and eloquent speakers. There befell al-Ghazali favorable opportunities from interacting with imams, encountering stubborn opponents, debating with eminent men, and contending with the great. His name became famous in the horizons, and he benefited thereby the utmost benefit, until the situation led him to be ordered to go to Baghdad to teach at the auspicious Nizamiyya school there. So he went there, and everyone was amazed by his teaching and disputation. He met none like himself, and after being the imam of Khurasan, he became the imam of Iraq. Then he examined the science of principles (usul) and had mastered it, so he authored works in it. He revived the school (madhhab) in jurisprudence and authored works in it. He refined the science of disagreement (khilaf) and also produced works in it. His entourage and rank rose in Baghdad until his entourage overwhelmed the great ones, the commanders, and the caliphal court. Then the matter turned from another direction: after his extensive study of the exact sciences and his practice of the books authored in them, he adopted the path of asceticism and devotion to God, left behind entourage, discarded what he had attained of rank, and busied himself with the means of piety and the provisions of the Hereafter. So he left what he was in and headed to the House of God Almighty and performed Hajj, then...
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage is a biographical account of Imam al-Ghazali from the transmission of Abd al-Ghafir al-Farisi. It details his early life, education, rise to prominence, and later spiritual transformation. The narrative begins by praising al-Ghazali's unparalleled eloquence, intelligence, and nature. It then describes his early study of jurisprudence in Tus under Ahmad al-Radhkani, followed by his move to Naysabur to study under Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni). He quickly excelled, surpassing his peers and becoming a leading figure even during his teacher's lifetime. The passage notes a subtle rivalry: al-Juwayni would not look directly at al-Ghazali due to his jealousy of Ghazali's superior speed and strength, though outwardly he praised him. After al-Juwayni's death, al-Ghazali left Naysabur and joined the court of Nizam al-Mulk, where his abilities in debate and eloquence earned him high favor. He was sent to Baghdad to teach at the Nizamiyya school, where he became the leading scholar of both Khurasan and Iraq. He authored major works in usul al-fiqh, fiqh, and khilaf, and his influence grew immensely. However, as the passage concludes, he underwent a dramatic spiritual change, abandoning worldly status for asceticism, devotion, and the pilgrimage to Mecca, marking a shift from scholarly prestige to otherworldly renunciation.

المدرسة النظامية
التزهد

Key Takeaway
Al-Ghazali's journey from worldly fame to spiritual renunciation illustrates his profound intellectual and spiritual evolution.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you achieve recognition or success in your field, do you find it draws you closer to God or distracts you? What specific step can you take this week to guard against pride at a moment of achievement?
?
Al-Ghazali's change came through deep study of the 'exact sciences' and spiritual practice. Is there an area of Islamic knowledge you have avoided that could revitalize your faith? Commit to exploring one such topic this month.
?
If you were at the peak of your career and felt an inner call to renounce it all, would you have the courage to follow? What attachments in your life would you find hardest to leave for the sake of your soul?

Life of Imam Ibn Asakir

Early Life and Studies of Imam al-Ghazali
Ibn 'Asakir narrates al-Ghazali's early education and his teacher's mixed reaction to his talent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the early academic formation of Imam al-Ghazali and his relationship with his teacher Imam al-Haramayn al-Juwayni.

Core Idea
Al-Ghazali's brilliance shone early, surpassing even his famous teacher.

This passage from Ibn 'Asakir's biography introduces us to Abu Hamid al-Ghazali as a young student. We see him in Tus and then in Nishapur under the great Imam al-Haramayn. His rapid progress and natural talent set him apart, but also created a complex dynamic with his teacher.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the passage carefully once to get a sense of the narrative.
2
Identify key events
Note the sequence: studying in Tus, moving to Nishapur, excelling, beginning to teach and write.
3
Examine the teacher-student relationship
Look for how Imam al-Haramayn reacted to al-Ghazali's abilities. The text mentions both outward pride and inner concealment.
This shows human nature even among great scholars.

، حتي أدت الحال به إلى أن رسم للمصير إلى بغداد للقيام بتدريس المدرسة الميمونة النظامية بها ، فصار إليها وأعجب الكل بتدريسه ومناظرته ، وما لقي مثل نفسه وصار بعد إمامة خراسان إمام العراق، ثم نظر في علم الأصول وكان قد أحكمها فصنف فيه تصانيف وجدد المذهب فى الفقه فصنف فيه تصانيف ، وسبك الخلاف فحرر فيه أيضاً تصانيف وعلت حشمته و درجته في بغداد ، حتى كان تغلب حشمته الأكابر والأمراء ودار الخلافة فانقلب The shaykh Abu al-Hasan 'Abd al-Ghafir ibn Isma'il al-Farisi informed us in his book, saying: Muhammad ibn Muhammad ibn Muhammad Abu Hamid al-Ghazali, the Proof of Islam and the Muslims, the Imam of the imams of religion, whose like eyes have not seen in tongue, eloquence, speech, intellect, intelligence, and disposition. He tasted a portion in his youth in Tus of fiqh under the Imam Ahmad al-Radh[?] Kani[?]; then he came to Nishapur attending the lessons of Imam al-Haramayn in a group of youths from Tus. He strove and exerted himself until he graduated in a short period, surpassed his peers, and memorized the Qur'an, and became the foremost of the people of his time and the unique one of his contemporaries in the days of Imam al-Haramayn. The students would benefit from him, and he would teach them, guide them, and strive in himself. His affair reached the point that he began authoring works. And the Imam [al-Haramayn]—despite his high rank, lofty expression, and rapid flow in speech and talk—would not incline his gaze toward al-Ghazali, out of concealing his superiority over him in speed of expression and strength of disposition, nor did he find it pleasant for him to undertake authoring, even though he was a graduate of his and attributed to him, as is not hidden from human nature. But outwardly he would show pride in him and reliance on his station, contrary to what he concealed. Then he remained thus until the end of the Imam's days. He left Nishapur and went to the military camp, and occupied from the council of Nizam al-Mulk a place of acceptance. The vizier turned to him due to his high rank, the appearance of his name, and the excellence of his debating and his flowing expression. That court was the resting place of scholars and the destination of imams and eloquent speakers. So al-Ghazali had good opportunities of mixing with imams, meeting obstinate opponents, debating brilliant men, and contending with great ones. His name became famous in the horizons, and he benefited thereby the most complete benefit, until the situation led to his being commissioned to go to Baghdad to undertake teaching at the blessed Nizamiyya school there. He went there, and everyone was amazed at his teaching and debating; he met no one like himself. After being the imam of Khurasan, he became the imam of Iraq. Then he studied the science of usul, which he had mastered, and authored works in it. He renewed the madhhab in fiqh and authored works in it. He refined the method of khilaf and also authored works in it. His retinue and rank rose in Baghdad until his retinue overcame the great ones, the princes, and the caliphal court. Then the matter turned from another aspect: after studying the subtle sciences and practicing the books authored therein, there appeared upon him asceticism and devotion to God (tazahhud wa ta'alluh). He abandoned retinue, cast off the rank he had attained, and occupied himself with the means of piety and the provision for the Hereafter. So he left what he was in, headed for the House of God (the Ka'ba), performed Hajj, then entered Syria and stayed in those lands for nearly ten years, touring the revered sacred sites. He undertook the famous works that none had preceded him in, such as 'Ihya' 'Ulum al-Din' and condensed books like 'al-Arba'in', and other treatises. Whoever ponders them knows the man's station in the various sciences. He undertook struggling against the soul (mujahadat al-nafs), ethics, improving character, and refining livelihood. So the devil of vanity, seeking leadership and status, and adorning oneself with blameworthy ethics turned into tranquility of soul, nobility of character, freedom from formalities and embellishments, adorning with the dress of the righteous, shortening of hope, dedicating of times to guiding creation and calling them to what helps them in the matter of the Hereafter, making the world and preoccupation with it hateful to wayfarers, preparation for departure to the everlasting abode, and submission to everyone in whom one perceives or from whom one smells a trace of knowledge (ma'rifa) and alertness to something of the lights of witnessing (mushahada), until he became accustomed to that. Then he returned to his homeland, remaining in his house, occupied with reflection, adhering to time, a sought-after precious treasure for hearts and for everyone who seeks him and enters upon him, until that lasted for a time. The works appeared and the books spread, and no contradiction to what he was upon appeared in his days, nor any objection by anyone to what he had left as legacy. Until the turn of the vizierate came to the noble Fakhr al-Mulk, Jamal al-Shuhada' (may God cover him with His mercy), and Khurasan was adorned with his retinue and state. He had heard and verified al-Ghazali's station, rank, perfection of virtue, condition, purity of creed, and purity of conduct. So he sought blessing from him, attended him, heard his speech, and requested that his breath and benefits not remain fruitless with no benefit taken from them or borrowing of their lights. He insisted with all insistence and was firm in the request until he agreed to go out and was brought to Nishapur. But the lion was absent from his den, and the matter was hidden in the concealed decree of God and His secret. So he was advised to teach at the blessed Nizamiyya school (may God preserve it). He found no alternative but to submit to the authorities, and he intended by manifesting what he was occupied with to guide the seekers and benefit those who come, not to return to what he had cast off and freed himself from the bondage of seeking status, disputing with peers, and contending with opponents. Many times they struck his staff [criticized him] with opposition, falling into [attacking] him, and slandering what he emitted and produced, and intriguing against him and defaming him, but he was not affected by that, nor did he occupy himself with answering the slanderers, nor did he show unease at the pinpricks of the mischief-makers. I visited him many times, and I did not sense in myself—given what I had known of him in former times of harshness, disturbing people, looking at them with disdain and belittling them out of pride, conceit, and vanity at what he had been given—
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage is a biography of Imam Abu Hamid al-Ghazali, narrated by 'Abd al-Ghafir al-Farisi. It begins by identifying al-Ghazali's full name and lauding him as the 'Proof of Islam' and the 'Imam of the imams of religion,' unmatched in eloquence and intellect. The account then describes his early studies in Tus under an Imam Ahmad (the name is slightly unclear in the OCR), followed by his move to Nishapur to attend the lessons of Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni). He excelled rapidly, becoming the foremost among his peers and even teaching other students while still a student himself. Despite his brilliance, Imam al-Haramayn felt some jealousy due to al-Ghazali's superior speed and natural talent, though outwardly he expressed pride in him. After al-Juwayni's death, al-Ghazali entered the court of Nizam al-Mulk, where he gained favor through his debating skill. He was then sent to Baghdad to teach at the Nizamiyya school, where he became the leading scholar of Iraq. He authored works in usul al-fiqh, renewed the Shafi'i madhhab, and wrote on khilaf. His status rose greatly, but then he underwent a spiritual transformation after studying the inner sciences. He abandoned his worldly position, performed Hajj, traveled to Syria for about ten years, and composed his great works like 'Ihya' 'Ulum al-Din' and 'al-Arba'in'. He focused on self-discipline, ethics, and guiding people toward the Hereafter. He then returned to Tus, remaining in seclusion until Fakhr al-Mulk, the vizier of Khurasan, persuaded him to return to teaching at the Nizamiyya in Nishapur. Al-Ghazali agreed but only with the pure intention of guiding students, not to regain status. The passage ends with the narrator's personal observation that al-Ghazali had completely changed from his earlier pride and harshness to humility and patience in the face of criticism.

Key Terms
حجة الإسلام
إمام الحرمين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Imam al-Haramayn was entirely supportive of al-Ghazali's authorship and rise.
Correction: The text states that al-Juwayni outwardly showed pride but inwardly concealed his feelings, and he did not like al-Ghazali writing, despite being his teacher.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how did Imam al-Haramayn feel about al-Ghazali's authoring works?
A) He strongly supported and praised it.
B) He felt threatened and tried to stop him.
C) He pretended to be proud but inwardly did not approve.
D) He was indifferent.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ghazali's exceptional talent was evident early, causing both pride and concealed jealousy from his teacher.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Has there been someone in your life whose talent made you feel insecure? In light of Imam al-Haramayn's concealed jealousy, what does your reaction reveal about your own heart, and what will you do today to purify your intentions towards others' gifts?
?
Al-Ghazali's teacher outwardly praised him but inwardly held back. Is there a person you publicly support while privately resenting their success? What step will you take this week to honestly celebrate someone else's achievement without envy?
?
When you see someone surpass you in knowledge or skill, do you seek to learn from them or do you subtly diminish their accomplishments? What specific thought will you replace your jealousy with, starting now, so that you become a person who rejoices in others' excellence?

Al-Ghazali's Rise to Prominence under Nizam al-Mulk
Ibn 'Asakir details al-Ghazali's career advancement and scholarly achievements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Ghazali rose to become the leading scholar of his time, gaining favor with Nizam al-Mulk and teaching in Baghdad.

Core Idea
From student to master: al-Ghazali became the imam of Iraq after starting as a student in Khurasan.

This section of the biography follows al-Ghazali after the death of Imam al-Haramayn. He enters the court of the powerful vizier Nizam al-Mulk, where his debating skill earns him a position. He is eventually sent to Baghdad to teach at the Nizamiyya, and his fame spreads across the Islamic world.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the passage starting from 'ثم بقى' and ending at 'دار الخلافة'.
2
Identify key transitions
Note the move from Nishapur to the military camp, the favor of Nizam al-Mulk, and the journey to Baghdad.
3
Recognize his scholarly output
The passage lists works in usul, fiqh, and khilaf. This shows the breadth of his expertise.
4
Appreciate his status
His retinue became so great that it even surpassed that of the caliph's court.

وكانت تلك الحضرة محط رحال العلماء ومقصد الأئمة والفصحاء فوقعت للغزالى إتفاقات حسنة من الاحتكاك بالأئمة ، وملاقاة الخصوم اللد ومناظرة الفحول ومناقرة الكبار وظهر أسمه في الآفاق وارتفق بذلك أكمل الارتفاق ، حتي أدت الحال به إلى أن رسم للمصير إلى بغداد للقيام بتدريس المدرسة الميمونة النظامية بها ، فصار إليها وأعجب الكل بتدريسه ومناظرته ، وما لقي مثل نفسه وصار بعد إمامة خراسان إمام العراق، ثم نظر في علم الأصول وكان قد أحكمها فصنف فيه تصانيف وجدد المذهب فى الفقه فصنف فيه تصانيف ، وسبك الخلاف فحرر فيه أيضاً تصانيف وعلت حشمته و درجته في بغداد ، حتى كان تغلب حشمته الأكابر والأمراء ودار الخلافة فانقلب الأمر من وجه آخر وظهر عليه بعد مطالعة للعلوم الدقيقة وممارسة الكتب المصنفة فيها ، وسلك طريق التزهد والتأله وترك الحشمة وطرح ما نال من الدرجة والاشتغال بأسباب التقوى وزاد الآخرة ، فخرج عما كان فيه وقصد بيت الله تعالى وحج ثم دخل الشام وأقام في تلك الديار قريبا من عشر سنين يطوف المشاهد المعظمة ، وأخذ فى التصانيف المشهورة التي لم يسبق إليها مثل : ( إحيا علوم الدين ) والكتب المختصرة منها مثل ( الأربعين النفس وكرم الأخلاق والفراغ عن الرسوم والتزيينات والتزيى بزي الصالحين وقصر الأمل ، ووقف الأوقات علي هداية الخلق ودعائهم إلى ما يعينهم من أمر الآخرة وتبغيض الدنيا والاشتغال بها على السالكين والاستعداد للرحيل إلى الدار الباقية والانقياد لكل من يتوسم فيه أو يشم منه رائحة المعرفة والتيقظ لشئ من أنوار المشاهدة ، حتي مرن على ذلك والان ثم عاد إلى وطنه لازما بيته مشتغلا بالتفكر ملازما للوقت مقصودا نفيسا وذخرا للقلوب ولكل من يقصده ويدخل عليه إلى أن أتى على ذلك مدة وظهرت التصانيف وفشت الكتب ، ولم تبد في أيامه مناقضة لما كان فيه ولا اعتراض لأحد على ما أثره ، حتى انتهت نوبة الوزارة إلي الأجل فخر الملك جمال الشهداء تغمده الله برحمته وتزينت خراسان بحشمته و دولته ، وقد سمع وتحقق بمكان الغزالي ودرجته وكمال فضله وحالته ، وصفاء عقيدته ونقاء سيرته فتبرك به وحضره وسمع كلامه فاستدعى منه أن لا يبقى أنفاسه ومماراة الأقران ومكابرة المعاندين ، وكم قرع عصاه بالخلاف والوقوع فيه والطعن فيما يذره ويأتيه ، والسعاية به والتشنيع عليه فما تأثر به ولا أشتغل بجواب الطاعنين ولا أظهر أستيحاشا بغميزة المخلطين ولقد زرته مرارا وما كنت أحدس في نفسي مع ما عهدته في سالف الزمان عليه من الزعارة وإيجاش الناس والنظر إليهم بعين الازدراء والاستخفاف بهم كبرا وخيلاء واعتزازا بما رزق من
The shaykh Abu al-Hasan 'Abd al-Ghafir ibn Isma'il al-Farisi informed us in his book, saying: Muhammad ibn Muhammad ibn Muhammad Abu Hamid al-Ghazali, the Proof of Islam and the Muslims, the Imam of the imams of religion, whose like eyes have not seen in tongue, eloquence, speech, intellect, intelligence, and disposition. He tasted a portion in his youth in Tus of fiqh under the Imam Ahmad al-Radh[?] Kani[?]; then he came to Nishapur attending the lessons of Imam al-Haramayn in a group of youths from Tus. He strove and exerted himself until he graduated in a short period, surpassed his peers, and memorized the Qur'an, and became the foremost of the people of his time and the unique one of his contemporaries in the days of Imam al-Haramayn. The students would benefit from him, and he would teach them, guide them, and strive in himself. His affair reached the point that he began authoring works. And the Imam [al-Haramayn]—despite his high rank, lofty expression, and rapid flow in speech and talk—would not incline his gaze toward al-Ghazali, out of concealing his superiority over him in speed of expression and strength of disposition, nor did he find it pleasant for him to undertake authoring, even though he was a graduate of his and attributed to him, as is not hidden from human nature. But outwardly he would show pride in him and reliance on his station, contrary to what he concealed. Then he remained thus until the end of the Imam's days. He left Nishapur and went to the military camp, and occupied from the council of Nizam al-Mulk a place of acceptance. The vizier turned to him due to his high rank, the appearance of his name, and the excellence of his debating and his flowing expression. That court was the resting place of scholars and the destination of imams and eloquent speakers. So al-Ghazali had good opportunities of mixing with imams, meeting obstinate opponents, debating brilliant men, and contending with great ones. His name became famous in the horizons, and he benefited thereby the most complete benefit, until the situation led to his being commissioned to go to Baghdad to undertake teaching at the blessed Nizamiyya school there. He went there, and everyone was amazed at his teaching and debating; he met no one like himself. After being the imam of Khurasan, he became the imam of Iraq. Then he studied the science of usul, which he had mastered, and authored works in it. He renewed the madhhab in fiqh and authored works in it. He refined the method of khilaf and also authored works in it. His retinue and rank rose in Baghdad until his retinue overcame the great ones, the princes, and the caliphal court. Then the matter turned from another aspect: after studying the subtle sciences and practicing the books authored therein, there appeared upon him asceticism and devotion to God (tazahhud wa ta'alluh). He abandoned retinue, cast off the rank he had attained, and occupied himself with the means of piety and the provision for the Hereafter. So he left what he was in, headed for the House of God (the Ka'ba), performed Hajj, then entered Syria and stayed in those lands for nearly ten years, touring the revered sacred sites. He undertook the famous works that none had preceded him in, such as 'Ihya' 'Ulum al-Din' and condensed books like 'al-Arba'in', and other treatises. Whoever ponders them knows the man's station in the various sciences. He undertook struggling against the soul (mujahadat al-nafs), ethics, improving character, and refining livelihood. So the devil of vanity, seeking leadership and status, and adorning oneself with blameworthy ethics turned into tranquility of soul, nobility of character, freedom from formalities and embellishments, adorning with the dress of the righteous, shortening of hope, dedicating of times to guiding creation and calling them to what helps them in the matter of the Hereafter, making the world and preoccupation with it hateful to wayfarers, preparation for departure to the everlasting abode, and submission to everyone in whom one perceives or from whom one smells a trace of knowledge (ma'rifa) and alertness to something of the lights of witnessing (mushahada), until he became accustomed to that. Then he returned to his homeland, remaining in his house, occupied with reflection, adhering to time, a sought-after precious treasure for hearts and for everyone who seeks him and enters upon him, until that lasted for a time. The works appeared and the books spread, and no contradiction to what he was upon appeared in his days, nor any objection by anyone to what he had left as legacy. Until the turn of the vizierate came to the noble Fakhr al-Mulk, Jamal al-Shuhada' (may God cover him with His mercy), and Khurasan was adorned with his retinue and state. He had heard and verified al-Ghazali's station, rank, perfection of virtue, condition, purity of creed, and purity of conduct. So he sought blessing from him, attended him, heard his speech, and requested that his breath and benefits not remain fruitless with no benefit taken from them or borrowing of their lights. He insisted with all insistence and was firm in the request until he agreed to go out and was brought to Nishapur. But the lion was absent from his den, and the matter was hidden in the concealed decree of God and His secret. So he was advised to teach at the blessed Nizamiyya school (may God preserve it). He found no alternative but to submit to the authorities, and he intended by manifesting what he was occupied with to guide the seekers and benefit those who come, not to return to what he had cast off and freed himself from the bondage of seeking status, disputing with peers, and contending with opponents. Many times they struck his staff [criticized him] with opposition, falling into [attacking] him, and slandering what he emitted and produced, and intriguing against him and defaming him, but he was not affected by that, nor did he occupy himself with answering the slanderers, nor did he show unease at the pinpricks of the mischief-makers. I visited him many times, and I did not sense in myself—given what I had known of him in former times of harshness, disturbing people, looking at them with disdain and belittling them out of pride, conceit, and vanity at what he had been given—
Source pages p. 224

Commentary
This passage is a biography of Imam Abu Hamid al-Ghazali, narrated by 'Abd al-Ghafir al-Farisi. It begins by identifying al-Ghazali's full name and lauding him as the 'Proof of Islam' and the 'Imam of the imams of religion,' unmatched in eloquence and intellect. The account then describes his early studies in Tus under an Imam Ahmad (the name is slightly unclear in the OCR), followed by his move to Nishapur to attend the lessons of Imam al-Haramayn (al-Juwayni). He excelled rapidly, becoming the foremost among his peers and even teaching other students while still a student himself. Despite his brilliance, Imam al-Haramayn felt some jealousy due to al-Ghazali's superior speed and natural talent, though outwardly he expressed pride in him. After al-Juwayni's death, al-Ghazali entered the court of Nizam al-Mulk, where he gained favor through his debating skill. He was then sent to Baghdad to teach at the Nizamiyya school, where he became the leading scholar of Iraq. He authored works in usul al-fiqh, renewed the Shafi'i madhhab, and wrote on khilaf. His status rose greatly, but then he underwent a spiritual transformation after studying the inner sciences. He abandoned his worldly position, performed Hajj, traveled to Syria for about ten years, and composed his great works like 'Ihya' 'Ulum al-Din' and 'al-Arba'in'. He focused on self-discipline, ethics, and guiding people toward the Hereafter. He then returned to Tus, remaining in seclusion until Fakhr al-Mulk, the vizier of Khurasan, persuaded him to return to teaching at the Nizamiyya in Nishapur. Al-Ghazali agreed but only with the pure intention of guiding students, not to regain status. The passage ends with the narrator's personal observation that al-Ghazali had completely changed from his earlier pride and harshness to humility and patience in the face of criticism.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think al-Ghazali's success was primarily due to his teacher's reputation.
Correction: The text shows he earned his position through his own abilities, especially his debating skill and knowledge.

Checkpoint
What was the immediate catalyst for al-Ghazali's rise in the court of Nizam al-Mulk?
A) His lineage from Imam al-Haramayn
B) His skill in debate and eloquence

D) His wealth and social connections

Key Takeaway
Al-Ghazali's debating skill and scholarship earned him the highest academic position of his time in Baghdad.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Ghazali's name rose because of his eloquence and debating skill. What skill or knowledge are you dedicating yourself to mastering right now, and are you willing to put in the intense effort it takes to become truly excellent?
?
He sought out the company of great imams and debated strong opponents. Who are the people in your life that challenge you intellectually and spiritually? If you lack such company, what will you do this week to find or create it?
?
His rank in Baghdad became so high that even princes deferred to him. When you achieve success, do you seek recognition for yourself, or do you use your position to serve Allah? What would need to change in your heart for you to answer that honestly?

Al-Ghazali's Spiritual Transformation and Later Years
Ibn 'Asakir recounts al-Ghazali's spiritual journey and final years.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the profound change in al-Ghazali's life after he abandoned worldly status for asceticism and how he returned to teaching with a new intention.

Core Idea
Al-Ghazali traded fame for devotion, becoming a guide for seekers.

This final portion of the biography describes a dramatic shift. After reaching the pinnacle of academic success, al-Ghazali turned away from worldly honors. He performed Hajj, traveled in Syria, and authored his most famous spiritual works. The passage also recounts his eventual return to teaching under the vizier Fakhr al-Mulk, but with a purified intention.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read from 'فانقلب الأمر من وجه آخر' to the end.
2
Identify the change
Notice the shift from worldly success to asceticism. The text mentions specific practices: tazahhud, ta'alluh, leaving his rank.
3
Note his works and practices
He wrote Ihya' 'Ulum al-Din and other works, and engaged in self-struggle (mujahada).
4
Understand his return to teaching
Under Fakhr al-Mulk, he agreed to teach but with the intention of guiding seekers, not for status.
5
Observe his changed character
The narrator describes how al-Ghazali had completely transformed from pride to humility.
